<div id="review_419215528" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/419215528" />
    <a title="Stenwjohnson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1440211-stenwjohnson"><img alt="Stenwjohnson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1222982025p2/1440211.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/419215528?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stenwjohnson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stenwjohnson" href="/user/show/1440211-stenwjohnson">Stenwjohnson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer419215528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7723830853163597006">“Do not call up that which you cannot put down.”<br/> — H. P. Lovecraft, "The Case of Charles Dexter Ward"<br/><br/>I first read H.P. Lovecraft’s 1927 short novel “The Case of Charles Dexter Ward” when I was 11 years old, in the tacky Del Rey edition pictured. At the time, I was surprised by the difference between the densely erudite content and the pulp cover art. A horror writer with high-literary roots, Lovecraft remains a unique and original artist; at his best, he combines the ethereal sensibilities of “d</span>
  <span id="freeText7723830853163597006" style="display:none">“Do not call up that which you cannot put down.”<br /> — H. P. Lovecraft, "The Case of Charles Dexter Ward"<br /><br />I first read H.P. Lovecraft’s 1927 short novel “The Case of Charles Dexter Ward” when I was 11 years old, in the tacky Del Rey edition pictured. At the time, I was surprised by the difference between the densely erudite content and the pulp cover art. A horror writer with high-literary roots, Lovecraft remains a unique and original artist; at his best, he combines the ethereal sensibilities of “decadent” fantasists like Lord Dunsany and Arthur Machen with the bleak gothic tales of Poe and Ambrose Bierce. My scholarship is a bit of cheat, since those are all known influences, but Lovecraft is also different from his peers in his obsession with abstract, implacable dread. He delights in a trademarked sensation of religious-epiphany-in-reverse; the fabric of reality, when explored too deeply, always yields inscrutable horror. Poe and Bierce are actually more reliant on the pulp mechanics of surprise endings and clockwork irony. Lovecraft is fonder of dark, cosmic irresolution. <br /><br />“The Case of Charles Dexter Ward” features a typical Lovecraftian hero, a young man in 1920s Providence, RI whose “antiquarian” obsessions lead to occult discoveries. By “antiquarian,” Lovecraft means (without a trace of irony) that Ward is preoccupied with the postage stamp-sized world of pre-Revolutionary Providence, with interests as disturbingly esoteric as Colonial wood-working techniques.<br /><br />Ward begins to research an ancestor, Joseph Curwen, whose identity was mysteriously effaced from town records and learns that he was a notorious practitioner of black magic. He begins his own series of experiments into the unknown, resulting in what appears to madness. Or is it? Or has his identity been taken by a figure from the past? Another Lovecraftian archetype, psychiatrist (or “alienist”) Dr. Willett investigates; his discoveries take him to inexplicable occult findings, a startling conclusion, and an epic battle of wills. <br /><br />It would be unfair to call Lovecraft’s style eccentric. It is urbane, precise, and boldly oblivious to contemporary literary trends. Defiantly anachronistic, with British spellings and mannerisms ("shew" anyone?) and an obsession with minute detail and rhetorical elegance, his prose can be detached to a fault: all the dialog in “Dexter Ward” could fit on the head of a pin, and most subjective expression occurs in letters and journal entries, some written in a brilliant, intentionally-enigmatic pastiche of 17th century prose. Lovecraft even makes a rare, satirical swipe at TS Eliot, and one can see the unlikely parallels between the two writers: buttoned-up early 20th century antiquarians chafing against modernity, shaking their fists at an opaque universe.<br /><br />Virtually unknown during his lifetime, Lovecraft has since gained literary respect and looms large in popular culture. But he is often misunderstood or willfully misread; think of the innumerable films that are based on his work in name alone (and “Charles Dexter Ward” was filmed by Roger Corman as the much-altered “The Haunted Palace”). Readers willing to accept his unusual technique will be rewarded by the prodigious, wildly unconventional imagination of an American genius. There is nothing like him.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7723830853163597006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3057336885" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3057336885" />
    <a title="Lena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35645863-lena"><img alt="Lena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412534810p2/35645863.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3057336885?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lena" href="/user/show/35645863-lena">Lena</a>
      </span>

<div id="review_44060784" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44060784" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1938544-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330120106p2/1938544.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44060784?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1938544-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Horror fans, sci fi fans, goths</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Michael by: </span>
      Dungeons and Dragons
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1938544-michael?shelf=pulp-fiction">pulp-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer44060784&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44060784" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10251812793134940332">Having just re-read this book as part of the goodreads HPL reading group, I feel I can revise my original review. The book was apparently not one of Lovecraft's favorites among his own work, and it was never published in his lifetime. It is the longest piece he ever wrote, and in various ways it reflects his discomfort with the novel, as opposed to his facility with the short story. There are redundancies, as if HPL himself found it difficult to remember what had already been said (or was unsure</span>
  <span id="freeText10251812793134940332" style="display:none">Having just re-read this book as part of the goodreads HPL reading group, I feel I can revise my original review. The book was apparently not one of Lovecraft's favorites among his own work, and it was never published in his lifetime. It is the longest piece he ever wrote, and in various ways it reflects his discomfort with the novel, as opposed to his facility with the short story. There are redundancies, as if HPL himself found it difficult to remember what had already been said (or was unsure what the reader would recall) and sections that seem to pad out the narrative unnecessarily. I still regard it as a masterpiece, perhaps all the more enjoyable for its flaws.<br /><br />The story involves a young antiquarian who discovers that he had an identical ancestor burned as a witch shortly before the Revolution (rather late for witch-hunting, as HPL knew full well). As his investigations continue, he seems to come under the spell of this long-dead sorceror, and begins to mimic his behaviors and interests. Ultimately, he discovers the formula to summon his ancestor from the dead, and the man kills him and attempts to replace him in life. All of this is witnessed through closed doors by the family and and the family doctor, who comes to understand what has happened only in pieces. The climax of the story comes when the young man's imposter is placed in an asylum (because he acts like someone from 150 years ago) and the doctor examines the underground lair of the magician - finding horrible secrets there.<br /><br />About the first third of the book is taken up with recounting the life of the sorceror, Joseph Curwen, in the 17th and 18th centuries, up to his extirpation from Providence by an angry mob of local and sailors. This part of the book Lovecraft evidently dismissed as an "indulgence in antiquarianism" but for a historian it is a pleasure to read. I wish that more vampire authors would take the time to painstakingly research the past as HPL does here, in order to give the backgrounds of their long-lived protagonists some depth. The story makes a somewhat questionable representation of the Lovecraft oeuvre, because there seems to be no true "cosmic threat" to humanity - just a lone scholar attempting to raise the dead. This theme did allow HPL to show off his occult erudition, however, and to insert some well-placed humor at the expense of the occultists of his day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10251812793134940332" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_76020351" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/76020351" />
    <a title="Printable Tire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59506-printable-tire"><img alt="Printable Tire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1226430554p2/59506.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76020351?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Printable Tire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Printable Tire" href="/user/show/59506-printable-tire">Printable Tire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76020351" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8399629247702779956">Being from Providence, I feel I have to like things associated with Providence, and thus be a H.P. Lovecraft fan (of course, I don't like Family Guy, but that's another story). Fortunately a story like this makes it easy to like him. It certainly helps that I know and walk the streets and locations mentioned in this book, read parts of the book in Salem where some of the story takes place, am a fellow alumni of the same school as the protagonist, and also, when I get lost in Cranston, feel inexp</span>
  <span id="freeText8399629247702779956" style="display:none">Being from Providence, I feel I have to like things associated with Providence, and thus be a H.P. Lovecraft fan (of course, I don't like Family Guy, but that's another story). Fortunately a story like this makes it easy to like him. It certainly helps that I know and walk the streets and locations mentioned in this book, read parts of the book in Salem where some of the story takes place, am a fellow alumni of the same school as the protagonist, and also, when I get lost in Cranston, feel inexplicably drawn to Pawtuxet Village. <br /><br />Lovecraft knows what to include and what to omit, and sets a respectable serious tone throughout (though I think I caught two quasi-intentional jokes). I was drawn in by the mystery and picking up of clues and the myth surrounding the secret society. I am very impressed by Lovecraft's ability to create his own mythology which runs throughout his various stories. He presents this particular story in such a way that while one might not actually think it is historical, there might be enough evidence, if one did the digging, to turn up mysterious happenings before the burning of the Gaspee and the older days of Providence.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8399629247702779956" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_186935380" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/186935380" />
    <a title="Bandit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4528663-bandit"><img alt="Bandit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289488158p2/4528663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/186935380?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bandit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bandit" href="/user/show/4528663-bandit">Bandit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer186935380" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer913682046053986255">I have such mixed feelings about this book, it was a very interesting and spooky story, but on the other hand reading it felt like work the entire way to the end. The denseness of prose, the utter lack of dialogue, the constant verboseness didn't lend themselves to an enjoyable read. I have to say I'm glad to have read it and I plan on reading Lovecraft in the future, though most likely sticking to his shorter works.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2202376428" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2202376428" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2202376428?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2202376428" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7146818401112794395"><b>Don't Rise Up what you Can't Put Down</b><br/>5 December 2017 - Sydney<br/><br/>	The first thing that I did when I started to read this book was to jump onto Facebook and search for a 'Charles Dexter Ward', Well, I found one, but he had his account locked down to tightly that you could only send him a message. Actually, I'm not at all that surprised because he probably gets lots of friendship requests (and messages) from Cthulu freaks out there. Actually, I'm almost tempted to set up an account on Facebook called </span>
  <span id="freeText7146818401112794395" style="display:none"><b>Don't Rise Up what you Can't Put Down</b><br />5 December 2017 - Sydney<br /><br />	The first thing that I did when I started to read this book was to jump onto Facebook and search for a 'Charles Dexter Ward', Well, I found one, but he had his account locked down to tightly that you could only send him a message. Actually, I'm not at all that surprised because he probably gets lots of friendship requests (and messages) from Cthulu freaks out there. Actually, I'm almost tempted to set up an account on Facebook called Charles Dexter Ward, and see how many people send me friend requests (or messages about how to do some Cthonian rituals). No, I have better things to do with my time, such as reading more Lovecraft.<br /><br />	Anyway, I'm currently sitting on one of Sydney's Ferries heading across the harbour so that I can finish the couple of pubs on the north side that I have yet to visit. Actually, these ferries are pretty cool as I found a table on which to rest my laptop, a power socket for my cord, and free wifi. However, I didn't bring my cable since I didn't expect to find such conveniences, and I won't be using the wifi since I'll probably be at my destination once I connect. It is still cool looking out of the window and seeing the Opera House, and a massive cruise liner that still baffles me as to why people spend extraordinary amounts of money to travel on (and the only reason I would go on a cruise liner would be to make an attempt to travel from Melbourne to London, or even to Capetown, without actually catching a plane, though apparently getting into Sudan by the land border is nigh impossible).<br /><br />	However, I probably should say something about this rather long short story because I doubt you are reading my reviews simply to find out what I am doing while writing it. Anyway, it is about a man, Charles Dexter Ward, who disappears from an insane asylum, and his doctor who attempts to get to the bottom of what is actually going on. Mind you, as is the case with most Lovecraft (though this is only the second that I have read), the one thing that you really don't want to do is to dig into things because what you end up uncovering is no doubt going to have a dramatic effect upon your mental health, if not actually kill you.<br /><br />	The story begins back in the 16th Century when a Joseph Curwen leaves Salem due to the onset of the witchhunts, and settles in Providence. Here he builds a house and continues his experiments, which involves at first the sacrifice of an awful lot of animals, and then humans beings in the form of slaves. After a while, the towns people become rather concerned, despite the fact that he is incredibly wealthy, and stage a raid on his house to discover some rather nasty creatures that he seems to be grooming, The creatures are killed, and the house, and Joseph, burnt to a crisp.<br /><br />	We then jump to the modern era, or the modern era where Lovecraft is concerned, and we have Charles discovering that Curwen is his ancestor, and then attempting to dig into what Currian was on about. Anyway, I don't really want to go too deep into the synopsis as there are some rather interesting twists in the story, but what it does involve is the good doctor descending into the labyrinth beneath Curwen's former house to actually get to the bottom of what it going on with Ward. As I also suggested, where Lovecraft is concerned this is not all that wise, and it always brings to mind the old Call of Cthulu roleplaying game which was designed to basically kill off your character, though not in the way that other games (such as Paranoia) did. The thing is that the world of H.P. Lovecraft is a very, very dangerous world.<br /><br />	A couple of things that I have noticed is that most of the stories seem to be set around New England. Actually, a lot of Stephen King's stories are also set in the same region. Sure, that has something to do with both them actually living in and around the area, but there is also something quite spooky about New England. First of all you have the hysteria of the Salem Witch Trials, though that was just a carry on from what had been occurring in the continent for centuries beforehand. Actually, it is interesting that we here more about the witch trials in Salem, and very little about what actually happened in the old world.<br /><br />	Thus, it is not surprising that Lovecraft connected Salem with his Cthulu Mythos, suggesting that the trials were not based on hysteria, or people attempting to get back at their enemies, but instead was actually based on the fact that people were dealing with the dark realms. However, these dark realms were the realms of Satan, but rather the realms of the Great Old Ones – a realm that if people knew about then they would surely go insance. Curwen, and Ward, are just one of the many cultists that are scattered across the region, and I have to admit that New England does come across as a very spooky place. While I could be wrong, but didn't the headless horseman roam this area? In another way it is the old world, or the old world at least where the United States are concerned. The cities here date back centuries, and the architecture and town planning is reflective of this. This place is a realm of dark secrets that go back to the time of the colonials, and even before. Remember, this was also where the persecuted religiousnists fled to start a new life.<br /><br />	The other interesting thing is the idea of controlling what you raise. This is a theme that seems to echo through the story – if you are going to dabble in the dark arts, make sure that you know what you are doing. How many people do stupid things and end up getting themselves killed. Well, I guess that is why we have the Darwin awards. Then again drugs and alcohol tend to have that effect on people – we're drunk so lets do something stupid. I'm not sure this is something that Lovecraft was trying to get across, but this is something that clearly came out at the end. Interestingly, this does actually apply to witchcraft as people do end up delving into the arena only to discover that it back fires – Dr Faust anyone?</span>
  <a data-text-id="7146818401112794395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_592278031" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/592278031" />
    <a title="Evan Leach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7713825-evan-leach"><img alt="Evan Leach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1384621842p2/7713825.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/592278031?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan Leach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan Leach" href="/user/show/7713825-evan-leach">Evan Leach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=1920-1929">1920-1929</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer592278031" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17268682748501183825"><i>”From a private hospital for the insane near Providence, Rhode Island, there recently disappeared an exceedingly singular person. He bore the name of Charles Dexter Ward, and was placed under restraint most reluctantly by the grieving father who had watched his aberration grow from a mere eccentricity to a dark mania involving both a possibility of murderous tendencies and a profound and peculiar change in the apparent contents of his mind.”</i><br/><br/><b>The Case of Charles Dexter Ward</b>, the longest work by H.</span>
  <span id="freeText17268682748501183825" style="display:none"><i>”From a private hospital for the insane near Providence, Rhode Island, there recently disappeared an exceedingly singular person. He bore the name of Charles Dexter Ward, and was placed under restraint most reluctantly by the grieving father who had watched his aberration grow from a mere eccentricity to a dark mania involving both a possibility of murderous tendencies and a profound and peculiar change in the apparent contents of his mind.”</i><br /><br /><b>The Case of Charles Dexter Ward</b>, the longest work by H.P. Lovecraft (and his only novel), is a good ol’ fashioned Lovecraftian tale: boy becomes fascinated by creepy and mysterious ancestor, boy dives into dark arcane rituals, and New England is subjected to unspeakable horrors. The story is mostly told through the perspective of Ward’s doctor, as he unravels the mystery of who Ward’s ancestor really was and what really happened with Charles, so I won’t dive deep into the plot and spoil any of the surprises. Suffice to say that the story is vintage Lovecraft, and if you’ve enjoyed his more famous works chances are you’ll like this one. The novel is chock full of Lovecraft’s signature prose, which has been known to turn some readers off but is manna from heaven (or hell, as the case may be) for HPL fans:<br /><br /><i>”It is hard to explain just how a single sight of a tangible object with measurable dimensions could so shake and change a man; and we may only say that there is about certain outlines and entities a power of symbolism and suggestion which acts frightfully on a sensitive thinker’s perspective and whispers terrible hints of obscure cosmic relationships and unnameable realities behind the protective illusions of common vision…he crawled and rolled desperately away over the damp pavement where dozens of Tartarean wells poured forth their exhausted whining and yelping to answer his own insane cries.”</i><br /><br />Good stuff.<br /><br />Now, I didn’t think this was H.P.’s strongest work. Lovecraft himself was a bit disappointed with this book, calling it a “cumbrous, creaking bit of self-conscious antiquarianism” and neglecting to publish it during his lifetime. The (relatively) long length of this story does give Lovecraft a little too much time to dwell on antiquarian matters, like colonial architecture, and there’s the occasional racist moment (a black cat named Nig, for example) that may make the book feel uncomfortably dated to modern readers. But the story picks up in the second half, and I did end up enjoying it. As I mentioned, if you like H.P.’s other work, chances are you will too. <b>3 stars</b>, recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17268682748501183825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1hAntNlZ9pFQaoNbBZTaupUHjHOckgM3+sU8StoL+f2mJPskUP3phF5bIhwse1EzKj+Q1xlIkX3qBB9/cyFqww==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('uK5K37w7aukZYOYPUIPhwOu6Tg12oySvak9G+4ikuZDImpZPNZ91/BdRR0h5bGpJVIJSqfN5tuV6jmXOIY4qrg==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/9494.H_P_Lovecraft">About H.P. Lovecraft</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/9494.H_P_Lovecraft"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1299165714p3/9494.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/9494.H_P_Lovecraft">H.P. Lovecraft
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
13,879
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17571338598841355296">Howard Phillips Lovecraft, of Providence, Rhode Island, was an American author of horror, fantasy and science fiction.<br/><br/>Lovecraft's major inspiration and invention was cosmic horror: life is incomprehensible to human minds and the universe is fundamentally alien. Those who genuinely reason, like his protagonists, gamble with sanity. Lovecraft has developed a cult following for his Cthulhu Mythos, a </span>
  <span id="freeText17571338598841355296" style="display:none">Howard Phillips Lovecraft, of Providence, Rhode Island, was an American author of horror, fantasy and science fiction.<br /><br />Lovecraft's major inspiration and invention was cosmic horror: life is incomprehensible to human minds and the universe is fundamentally alien. Those who genuinely reason, like his protagonists, gamble with sanity. Lovecraft has developed a cult following for his Cthulhu Mythos, a series of loosely interconnected fictions featuring a pantheon of human-nullifying entities, as well as the Necronomicon, a fictional grimoire of magical rites and forbidden lore. His works were deeply pessimistic and cynical, challenging the values of the Enlightenment, Romanticism and Christianity. Lovecraft's protagonists usually achieve the mirror-opposite of traditional gnosis and mysticism by momentarily glimpsing the horror of ultimate reality.<br /><br />Although Lovecraft's readership was limited during his life, his reputation has grown over the decades. He is now commonly regarded as one of the most influential horror writers of the 20th Century, exerting widespread and indirect influence, and frequently compared to Edgar Allan Poe.<br /><br />— <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H._P._Lovecraft" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17571338598841355296" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
